<span id="freeTextContainer17552664209559613056">Per the proposed new GR rating system: 4 goatheads; 4 pentagramatical swords; color: heliotrope!<br/><br/> *<br/><br/>Goddamn, look at me feeling all sentimental! Maybe it’s because I hadn’t read this book in nearly 20-years, or because I’ve just generally been on a Southern Gothic kick as of late, but I’m giving this bastard a grade it really doesn’t deserve. Why? Why not! It’s just all so much fun, so obviously intended to pick up the impossible legacy that Flannery O’Connor left behind. Had O’Connor’s preternatu</span>
  <span id="freeText17552664209559613056" style="display:none">Per the proposed new GR rating system: 4 goatheads; 4 pentagramatical swords; color: heliotrope!<br /><br /> *<br /><br />Goddamn, look at me feeling all sentimental! Maybe it’s because I hadn’t read this book in nearly 20-years, or because I’ve just generally been on a Southern Gothic kick as of late, but I’m giving this bastard a grade it really doesn’t deserve. Why? Why not! It’s just all so much fun, so obviously intended to pick up the impossible legacy that Flannery O’Connor left behind. Had O’Connor’s preternaturally-attenuated hand penned the same story, it would have been one for the ages—this breed of Southern arboreal satyr was her default tonic key, F(uckingNuts)#maj. Make no mistake: McCarthy is stealing from the Mistress here and the fruit is largely ripe. That isn’t meant as a swipe; this was the last necessary growing pain that enabled <i>Suttree</i>, and McCarthy with it, to emerge so masterfully a few more blood drops down that willow-canopied road.<br /><br />McCarthy does come up with some fantastic framing devices, interstitial chapterettes that read like bad true crime television interviews (‘I mean, shoot, Wilbur and Wilma loved my Jell-O molds…and boiling people alive, apparently.’) There’s no point getting into the plot, as this review is already threatening to have a higher word count than the novel itself (let’s just say Random House paginated <i>generously</i>). You honestly could have read twenty-five percent of the book by the time you finish this sentence. Now twenty-seven percent.<br /><br />So I’ll say this: if you think, like I certainly do, that more books could benefit from hillbilly ghouls ticking <i>scratchscratchscratch</i> against the lens of your eyeball with the yellowed filepoints of their fingertips, then <i>Child of God</i> be with you. But first ask yourself this: are you prepared to never conceptualize stuffed animals innocently again? Good. Then I leave you with the greatest metaphysical/ontological question that the inestimable and epoch-defining German philosopher Glenn-heinz Danzig challenged the 20th century with: “<i>Mommy, Can I Go Out and Kill Tonight</i>?”<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17552664209559613056" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1534844888" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1534844888" />
    <a title="Lauren" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25223589-lauren"><img alt="Lauren" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588473921p2/25223589.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1534844888?book_show_action=true">Dec 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lauren" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lauren" href="/user/show/25223589-lauren">Lauren</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330510950" href="/book/show/7616841-child-of-god">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25223589-lauren?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1534844888" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15553480305945233786"><i>You think people was meaner then than they are now? the deputy said.<br/>The old man was looking out at the flooded town. No, he said, I don't. I think people are the same from the day God first made one.</i><br/><br/>Never has madness been coincided with so fitting an atmosphere. A representation of fizzled humanity and self-preservation is often regarded as morally inept. But there's no doubt that our hero is Ballard, romping through a desolate landscape with his "dark lusts," poisoning Tennessee with his indulg</span>
  <span id="freeText15553480305945233786" style="display:none"><i>You think people was meaner then than they are now? the deputy said.<br />The old man was looking out at the flooded town. No, he said, I don't. I think people are the same from the day God first made one.</i><br /><br />Never has madness been coincided with so fitting an atmosphere. A representation of fizzled humanity and self-preservation is often regarded as morally inept. But there's no doubt that our hero is Ballard, romping through a desolate landscape with his "dark lusts," poisoning Tennessee with his indulgences. And yet this barely-human, almost troll-like figure has no intent to make a statement. He is only usurping from society satiation for himself. <br /><br />When it begins, our protagonist, "a child of God much like yourself perhaps," has been shed of his land, his home, and as the novel progresses as does his descent into the pits of the mind, drawn from the built world not only physically, as he isolates himself further and further geographically, but mentally. From the veins of leaves to the red clay mud on snow, the environment seems more nurturing and/or menacing to Ballard than any of the characters; not that Ballard himself is any creature worth praise. But isolation has always been a topic of interest for me, and carrying a book with little interaction between characters seems a bore, but when the world is described with the menacing and enchanting pen of McCarthy, there's no need for consistent dialogue. When it is sparsely applied, it's nerve-wracking and hard-hitting, regardless of how simple.<br /><br />For example:<br /><i>He's alive.<br />She watched him. He spooned up some applesauce and looked at it and put it down again. He opened the carton of milk and drank from it.<br />You don't really care one way or the other do you? she said.<br />Yes I do, said Ballard. I wish the son of a bitch was dead.</i><br /><br />On technicalities, McCarthy, as usual, abandons the conventions of writing, ridding his work of quotations or names in many cases. It does not confuse, it declutters, and in this instance, almost sets a tone. Of immediacy. Of purpose and need, and not names or faces that Ballard does not care about.<br /><br />My favorite scene in particular is one in which Ballard is purchasing food on layaway, the shopkeep saying that it'll take a hundred and four years to pay back what Ballard owes at the rate in which he's piling on his debt. It's our first look into Ballard's surprising youth, and his blunt, almost humorous exchange sets in something almost likable about him, regardless of, or perhaps due to, his flippancy.<br /><br />Like <i>The Road,</i> the land is hostile and giving in equal amounts, but nearing the end, there's a terrifying scene of claustrophobia and darkness so intense that the madness culminates not in the actions of people, but in the collapsing of the psyche. You can almost emulate the stilted breath and encapsulating walls, so nerve-wracking the reader, no matter how open her space is, might hyperventilate. But our madman works routinely and meticulously and vacantly at riddance of his limitations. And in the open world again, nature once again becomes a relief. Locations are absolutely central to the work, and a writer has never proven himself more masterful than McCarthy at describing the vividness and allegorical implications that the world surrounding a man creates.<br /><br /><i>He'd laid his finger open and sat with it in his mouth, the earth's rusty taste mingled with the ironrust tincture of blood. Dry dirt sifted down from the hole. He could see treelimbs against the sky. [...] He cast about among the stars for some kind of guidance but the heavens wore a different look that Ballard did not trust. He crossed through the woods and climbed a fence and crossed through a field until he came to a road. It was no place he'd ever stood in before. Seeing that uphill it led towards the mountains he took the other way and soon was hobbling along weak but able, the night being as fine as you could wish and a faint bloom of honeysuckle already on the air.</i><br /><br />Simplistic and evocative, dreamy and savage, the transportation into the mind of Ballard is disturbing and poignant in its misunderstanding of basic human conventions. Going through the robotic motions of survival, McCarthy's Ballard is a man and a creature unforgettable, and Tennessee is a landscape hazy and shadowed, lurking with undertones of violence, betraying and nursing and secretive.<br /><br /><i>Child of God</i> is a novel to be hallowed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15553480305945233786" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_115794528" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/115794528" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1036930-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583425448p2/1036930.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/115794528?book_show_action=true">Aug 08, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/1036930-michael">Michael</a>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780844667508" href="/book/show/46508.Child_of_God">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1036930-michael?shelf=1970s">1970s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1036930-michael?shelf=literature">literature</a>,

<span id="freeTextContainer1995957653024414643">My review just got completely deleted. I'm starting over, and it will be EVEN BETTER maybe. <br/><br/>There's a creek that winds along behind my parents house in downtown Indianapolis. It's barely more than a trickle, barely enough to get your feet wet when you're wearing shoes with thick soles. It was just wide enough that my ten year old self had to jump to make it across. But it was the wildest, most natural thing I'd known at that age, and I followed that son of a bitch. <br/><br/>I walked down one direction to</span>
  <span id="freeText1995957653024414643" style="display:none">My review just got completely deleted. I'm starting over, and it will be EVEN BETTER maybe. <br /><br />There's a creek that winds along behind my parents house in downtown Indianapolis. It's barely more than a trickle, barely enough to get your feet wet when you're wearing shoes with thick soles. It was just wide enough that my ten year old self had to jump to make it across. But it was the wildest, most natural thing I'd known at that age, and I followed that son of a bitch. <br /><br />I walked down one direction to where it reached a sewage pipe. (This review will get gross. Let me warn you now.) Not to be deterred, I bought a flashlight from the grocery store and walked through the pipe for a long time, finally coming out on the other side of a busy road. Feeling accomplished, I walked back overground, knowing where my next adventure along the creek would start.<br /><br />That was the pattern: I'd bike to the last place where I'd followed the creek, hide my bike, and then continue following. It became more and more time consuming as it wound farther and farther from our backyard. It became wider, wilder, as it went. One day, I came to an old tool box buried in the dirt. I dug it up, hoping to find some buried treasure, or at least some forgotten toys some other kid had buried. All I found inside was water and dirt, but I returned awhile later to put some treasure in the box: some old toys I didn't play with anymore. <br /><br />Eventually, I wandered down that creek far enough to reach the end in one direction. The narrow creek had gradually expanded by that point to ten or eleven feet across, but now it blossomed into a tremendous lake. Or so it seemed at the time. This small lake was surrounded by high grass, trees, some weed-like flowers, and a light dusting of garbage from downstream and from the nearest roads. You could see two busy roads far in the distance, but I wanted some private piece of nature bad enough that it felt to me like a nature preserve. I spent about half of the day exploring around the lake, and got in a shitstorm of trouble when I got back home. According to Mom, that wasn't a "lake" I'd been exploring; it was a "gravel pit," which was very "dangerous," and I should never go back. <br /><br />I went back. <br /><br />If I had to explain my love for McCarthy's writing, I don't know that I could. I could tell you that, ever since childhood, I've prefered the cracked and broken to the new and shiny. I could tell you I've written dozens of poems about moss and gravestones, and am uninterested in writing about anything much more complicated. It's an ingrained aesthetic, and I don't know what childhood trauma or early experience lies behind it, but it carries over into the books I read, the colors I like, and just about everything.<br /><br />Anyway, to conclude my review of the book: Cormac McCarthy is me. Lester Ballard, his protagonist, is the river. Lester has gravel pits and leaves a nasty taste in your mouth, and he's not there to make you happy, but McCarthy pursues his dark character so perfectly that you kind of end up feeling for the backwoods pervert by the end of it all. (The river/Ballard is the pervert, not McCarthy/me.) <br /><br />This is yet another terrific book by McCarthy, and I would recommend it to anyone interested in reading about depraved backwoods hilljacks who do just about everything gross imaginable. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1995957653024414643" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/35999712?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dayna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dayna" href="/user/show/1648192-dayna">Dayna</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8914318163661694871">It's not as clear of a narrative, like <em>No Country</em>, it's kind of like watching a train wreck in slow motion. The feeling of dread that builds throughout the book is excruciating - similar to the way I felt about Chigurh. <br/><br/>The writing style is very sparse - the descriptions of nature are poetic, in contrast to the ugliness of some of the action. I don't want to say too much about what happens, but it is truly shocking. The story starts out with the town auctioning off Lester Ballard's property - he</span>
  <span id="freeText8914318163661694871" style="display:none">It's not as clear of a narrative, like <em>No Country</em>, it's kind of like watching a train wreck in slow motion. The feeling of dread that builds throughout the book is excruciating - similar to the way I felt about Chigurh. <br /><br />The writing style is very sparse - the descriptions of nature are poetic, in contrast to the ugliness of some of the action. I don't want to say too much about what happens, but it is truly shocking. The story starts out with the town auctioning off Lester Ballard's property - he has defaulted on payment and the whole town has come out to see it sold, like a carnival. The town is excited and are basically eating up the entertainment of the auctioneer while this man's livelihood is being taken away and he is now homeless.<br /><br />The bottomless pit of loneliness that this man, Ballard, lives in is deeply sad and hopeless. His desperate attempts to have some kind of connection with other human beings ultimately drives him insane. The cruelty displayed toward him is thoughtless, and you can actually see what drives him to do what he does.<br /><br />There are some amusing asides that remind me of the stories that the sheriff (Tommy Lee Jones character) tells in <em>No Country </em>- little nuggets of life in a nothing little town - that are great.<br /><br />Overall, I think this is a great book - it made me feel an immense amount of compassion for this man - some might think he is a monster, but you see how everything played out, you see what was driving him to that point in his life.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8914318163661694871" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8446751351490511144">My least favorite novel from Cormac McCarthy thus far, so thanks be that it was short. Short, nasty and brutish. Set in the south, it features yet another evil madman such as The Judge from Blood Meridian or Chigurh from No Country For Old Men but with less lyricism, more necrophilia (yes, you heard that right and have been warned). But just in case you wonder if McCarthy thinks things are getting worse than ever, he has one cop ask another that very question and the guy responds no, human natur</span>
  <span id="freeText8446751351490511144" style="display:none">My least favorite novel from Cormac McCarthy thus far, so thanks be that it was short. Short, nasty and brutish. Set in the south, it features yet another evil madman such as The Judge from Blood Meridian or Chigurh from No Country For Old Men but with less lyricism, more necrophilia (yes, you heard that right and have been warned). But just in case you wonder if McCarthy thinks things are getting worse than ever, he has one cop ask another that very question and the guy responds no, human nature has always been like this. In other words, people like this have always been around.<br /><br />Oh, McCarthy is probably the greatest living American writer, and the prose is pbviously good, but tp what end? It is just quite possible that with the reading of Jo Nesbo and this that I have had my fill for the month of crazy, depraved, sexually deviant serial killers, but if you are due for one, have at itm, be my guest. I am just not that eager to delve into the mind of psychopathia, even one set in the moral universe of McCarthy. Please, I need a heartwarming picturebook or a funny book to try to scrub my mind from what I just read.</span>

<div id="review_745353159" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/745353159" />
    <a title="Bojan Gacic" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5799774-bojan-gacic"><img alt="Bojan Gacic" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1380028383p2/5799774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/745353159?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bojan Gacic" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bojan Gacic" href="/user/show/5799774-bojan-gacic">Bojan Gacic</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer745353159" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7477246103022503796">While browsing the Internet I stumbled upon a comment : ''Didn't like Child of God, couldn't get comfortable with it''. Rape, murder, necrophilia? Who could be/get comfortable with any of it? One could claim it is only instinctive and usual to flinch at the mere sight of such perversions- even if they remain descriptive in nature.<br/><br/>Thinking even further.......McCarthy.......when were any of you comfortable with his work? In here lays the core of our preference for his distinctive narrative. It is </span>
  <span id="freeText7477246103022503796" style="display:none">While browsing the Internet I stumbled upon a comment : ''Didn't like Child of God, couldn't get comfortable with it''. Rape, murder, necrophilia? Who could be/get comfortable with any of it? One could claim it is only instinctive and usual to flinch at the mere sight of such perversions- even if they remain descriptive in nature.<br /><br />Thinking even further.......McCarthy.......when were any of you comfortable with his work? In here lays the core of our preference for his distinctive narrative. It is impossible to remain ''in the middle''- you either grasp the gruesomeness and machoisms submerged in Southern Gothic, or pick something more balanced. I'm not judging, far from it, not all wish to spend most of the reading time in apprehension- holding onto the dictionary, and thinking: ''What the hell does this mean?''. That's the story with Cormac, every page holds a novel unexpectedness, be it a new word or the latest bloodthirsty detail. After reading most of his work, my mind has reached a conclusion as to why I'm an enthusiastic McCarthy admirer. The answer would be humbleness. Words, paragraphs, even pages go on without me being able to fully understand what every aspect of his writing represents. We can only be in awe of his skill and ability to manipulate language, and turn ordinary scenery into exceptional blends of violence and gore. However, in spite of the perpetual mystifications, the message remains clear- human degradation in its purest form.<br /><br />''Child of God'', although far from his finest work, even now, exactly four decades after publication, possesses more imagination than most national, international, multi-trillion bestsellers of our time. Yes, the violence is ever present, when you reach a point reasoning that this is as bad as it gets, McCarthy will cross the line with such blasé- take reason out of the equation, or forget ''Child of God'' .<br /><br />Cormac McCarthy has a trademark ability to elicit empathy for his protagonists no matter how terrible their actions are. <br /><br />-''A child of God much like yourself perhaps''. - <br /><br />From the start he cautions the reader not to inflict judgment, for we may never have the experience to settle in our minds how such a persona came to be molded. Lester Ballard- murderer, rapist, madman, threat to society, etc. Labels,tags, classifications, every single one of the aforementioned conveniently puts Ballard into dismissive context. Certainly, he is one sick fuck who needs to be dealt with. One question, though. How did he come to be such a vile, unsavory character? It will not take much for you to perceive the profound loneliness hindering his narrow soul constrained by personal history and circumstances. All of us turn and run at the mere sight of him, yet one must wonder how humanity is turning out to be preponderated by deviance. By all means, point fingers. Just not at the victimized individuals.<br /><br />There are Ballards roaming everywhere. The decadence of society-finally admitted by the media and the ruling political mechanisms- it was extant for quite a while, but not spoken of. Read ''Child of God'' and don't turn your head when it gets ugly. Why should you, it tells the absolute truth.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7477246103022503796" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9160240739019260164">My god, this is dark. Whereas in his later novels, McCarthy explores ideas of right and wrong, morality and justice, there is no trace of these in <i>Child of God</i>: only a sheer plunge to the depths of human depravity; an intimate confrontation with the worst that a man can become. And despite the grim subject matter, this is McCarthy at his most lyrical and poetic. He manages to wring such depth and colour from his words, creating vivid pictures from unusual and imaginative combinations. These imag</span>
  <span id="freeText9160240739019260164" style="display:none">My god, this is dark. Whereas in his later novels, McCarthy explores ideas of right and wrong, morality and justice, there is no trace of these in <i>Child of God</i>: only a sheer plunge to the depths of human depravity; an intimate confrontation with the worst that a man can become. And despite the grim subject matter, this is McCarthy at his most lyrical and poetic. He manages to wring such depth and colour from his words, creating vivid pictures from unusual and imaginative combinations. These images are beautiful, twisted, chilling and yet surprisingly intimate, personal and even comic, in a very morbid way. This is writing that pushes the boundaries. It is confronting, it is uncomfortable, and it stays with you. </span>

<div id="review_3291529993" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3291529993" />
    <a title="Bob Brinkmeyer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34401683-bob-brinkmeyer"><img alt="Bob Brinkmeyer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580600423p2/34401683.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3291529993?book_show_action=true">Apr 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bob Brinkmeyer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bob Brinkmeyer" href="/user/show/34401683-bob-brinkmeyer">Bob Brinkmeyer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3291529993" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6883424144470162884">At the start of the semester when I’m going over with a class the books we will be reading, I always pause and take a breath before I give a sentence or two about Child of God. “This is a book about a man named Lester Ballard who loses his farm and ends up killing people and sleeping with the bodies,” I tell my students. I give them a second to digest this summary, and then continue, “but it’s much more complex than that and it’s gorgeously written.” Then I ask who wants to be the discussion lea</span>
  <span id="freeText6883424144470162884" style="display:none">At the start of the semester when I’m going over with a class the books we will be reading, I always pause and take a breath before I give a sentence or two about Child of God. “This is a book about a man named Lester Ballard who loses his farm and ends up killing people and sleeping with the bodies,” I tell my students. I give them a second to digest this summary, and then continue, “but it’s much more complex than that and it’s gorgeously written.” Then I ask who wants to be the discussion leader for Child of God. Rarely does anyone volunteer, though by the end of the semester most of my students have become enthralled with McCarthy.<br /><br />Were this merely the tale of a demented necrophiliac—and on the surface that’s just what it is—there would be little to talk about other than McCarthy’s hypnotic prose, which is at once matter-of-fact and poetic, often with luminous bursts take your breath away, as when a pack of hunting dogs descend upon a boar: “Ballard watched this ballet tilt and swirl and churn mud up through the snow and watched the lovely blood welter there in its holograph of battle, spray burst from a ruptured lung, the dark heart’s blood, pinwheel and pirouette, until shots rang out and all was done.” Or this description of Ballard as he carries the corpse of one of his victims: “Scuttling down the mountain with the thing on his back he looked like a man beset by some ghast succubus, the dead girl riding him with legs bowed akimbo like a monstrous frog.” Or, one of my favorite lines from all of McCarthy: “Were there darker provinces of night he would have found them.” Scary—both scary in terms of what it says about Ballard and scary good in what it says about McCarthy as a writer. In some ways Child of God is a turning point for McCarthy, the novel in which he moves away from the overly heavy influence of Faulkner in his previous novels to discover his own voice, one that still has Faulknerian elements but that moves toward (but not completely into) a simpler, more direct expression.<br /><br />What makes this novel so rich and complex is that McCarthy challenges the reader not merely to dismiss Ballard as someone given to madness but to ponder why he slides toward that madness. Haunting the novel is a line that comes very early, the narrator describing Ballard as “a child of God much like yourself perhaps.” It’s a wonderful line, particularly the “perhaps,” because this is what the reader is forced to consider all along: Could I perhaps be someone like Ballard if I suddenly lost everything, was homeless and destitute? That Ballard might actually deserve some sympathy is hinted at throughout the novel, particularly in the community’s repeated rejecting and scapegoating of him, all the while that the community, as McCarthy lets us know, acts in ways every bit (well, almost) as violent as Ballard does, only the community’s violence is sanctioned, authorized. Even Ballard comes to see this when after being verbally abused by the sheriff, he responds: “You kindly got henhouse ways yourself, Sheriff,” which is true, the sheriff in several instances acting not unlike Lester, only he wears a badge.<br /><br />For all its violence, this is a deeply moving and sensitive portrayal of a man devolving toward being a caveman (Ballard eventually comes to live deep underground in a grotto). But in McCarthy’s world devolving wouldn’t be right: as Ballard becomes progressively dispossessed, falling further and further away from society and its restraints, his—and for McCarthy, humanity’s—true nature does not devolve but emerges. Perhaps.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6883424144470162884" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3340638441?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amanda NEVER MANDY" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amanda NEVER MANDY" href="/user/show/36873041-amanda-never-mandy">Amanda NEVER MANDY</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36873041-amanda-never-mandy?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36873041-amanda-never-mandy?shelf=cormac-and-cheese">cormac-and-cheese</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3340638441" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12468774034890918455"><b>
  <i>This is the story of a crusty old man and his travels across the countryside. Life keeps shitting on him, but he is a massive shit himself. Which came first, it is up for debate.</i>
</b><br/><br/>This was an interesting read. My opinion of the lead character, Lester Ballard, changed as each chapter gave up more of his story. What started as pity, quickly changed to disgust as his nefarious thoughts and actions were revealed. To know which came first, him being an awful person or life being awful to him was so</span>
  <span id="freeText12468774034890918455" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>This is the story of a crusty old man and his travels across the countryside. Life keeps shitting on him, but he is a massive shit himself. Which came first, it is up for debate.</i>
</b><br /><br />This was an interesting read. My opinion of the lead character, Lester Ballard, changed as each chapter gave up more of his story. What started as pity, quickly changed to disgust as his nefarious thoughts and actions were revealed. To know which came first, him being an awful person or life being awful to him was something I pondered quite a bit. It made me think of Nature vs. Nature and all that comes with it. Do we start life as a clean slate and become what we are based on what we experience or are we a predefined being that reacts to situations using the factory installed traits we are given?<br /><br />The last book I read from this author was a toe dip into his version of the violent and grotesque. This one was a full step off the ladder into waist deep muck. It is not that I have a weak stomach for it but more in that it is not my shot of vodka. This is book two of my 2020 clear the shelves of anything Cormac reading fest. If any from what I have left to read attempts to take me deeper into the sludge, I will have to climb out and take a break. A little bit of this type of thing can go a long way under normal life conditions when you have some happy thoughts saved up to counterbalance it. We are for sure not living normal right now and my resources are tapped out.<br /><br />Four stars to a book that made me think a lot about people and why they do what they do.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12468774034890918455" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1027316908" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1027316908" />
    <a title="Kamil" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23253765-kamil"><img alt="Kamil" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1381661393p2/23253765.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1027316908?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kamil" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kamil" href="/user/show/23253765-kamil">Kamil</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1797333583" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1797333583" />
    <a title="Doug H" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14987742-doug-h"><img alt="Doug H" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535994893p2/14987742.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1797333583?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Doug H" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Doug H" href="/user/show/14987742-doug-h">Doug H</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1797333583" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12646304015544289113"><br/><b>Sevier County, Tennessee is the Happiest Place on Earth. Bring Your Family. Go Spelunking!</b><br/><br/><i>“You think people was meaner back then than they are now? the deputy said. The old man was looking out at the flooded town. No, he said. I don’t. I think people are the same from the day God first made one.”</i><br/><br/>People are bad to the bone. Always have been, always will be. Or so Cormac McCarthy seems to feel. I don’t think I’ve read a more misanthropic book in my life. <br/><br/>I thoroughly enjoyed <i>The Road</i> and I liked t</span>
  <span id="freeText12646304015544289113" style="display:none"><br /><b>Sevier County, Tennessee is the Happiest Place on Earth. Bring Your Family. Go Spelunking!</b><br /><br /><i>“You think people was meaner back then than they are now? the deputy said. The old man was looking out at the flooded town. No, he said. I don’t. I think people are the same from the day God first made one.”</i><br /><br />People are bad to the bone. Always have been, always will be. Or so Cormac McCarthy seems to feel. I don’t think I’ve read a more misanthropic book in my life. <br /><br />I thoroughly enjoyed <i>The Road</i> and I liked this one well enough too but it didn’t grab me the way I was expecting it would. Despite its high shock value, I was left feeling fairly unmoved at the end. I’m not exactly sure why. I do admire McCarthy’s style a lot and it’s on firm display here, but the story itself felt somehow thin and padded at the same time and there wasn’t enough depth to the characters for me to latch on to them. I guess my main issue is that I wanted to get to know the protagonist better. Talk to me Lester. Why all of this mess? Did something go horribly wrong with your potty training? Was Piper Laurie your mommy? What?<br /><br />If those last few sentences make it sound like there’s poo poo play in this novel, don’t worry, there isn’t. But that’s about the only depravity you can think of that isn’t. Lester Ballard has a lot of issues.<br /><br />My favorite thing about this novel was its unexpected humor. No one told me this book was funny. Mostly I was just grinning quietly at how over-the-top some of the gore is, but some of it is actually funny and fun. I’ll close with two examples. <br /><br />Nervous grin: <i>“Gray soapy clots of matter fell from the cadaver’s chin. She ascended dangling. She sloughed in the weem of the noose. A gray rheum dripped.”</i><br /><br />Actual laugh: <i>“The dumpkeeper had spawned nine daughters and named them out of an old medical dictionary gleaned from the rubbish he picked. … Urethra, Cerebella, Hernia Sue.” </i><br /><br />3.5 Stars rounded down.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12646304015544289113" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_50274'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/50274.The_Room"><img alt="The Room" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1388962283l/50274._SX98_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('JoWqTgINrbhzuhBcWlMXyT2aGONi+g32tIiKZRbdHbFrBWv5D23rMYPyNe/YQemF7Er/KoCw9DFXKSggAncwkQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('r62pb9ajerhBsIluHGXAPFK/pokk8DelUHVLvDxwGSjiLWjY28M8MbH4rN2edz5wg29BQMa6zmKz1On5KNo0CA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

As a boy, he lives to decipher the odors of Paris, and apprentices himself to a prominent perfumer who teaches him the ancient art of mixing precious oils and herbs. But Grenouille\'s genius is such that he is not satisfied to stop there, and he becomes obsessed with capturing the smells of objects such as brass doorknobs and fresh-cut wood. After losing her job when the Garmin factory shuts down, Nonie is left with major money issues—as in, she has none. Desperate, she has to turn to the one place she’s avoided working since dropping out of college--her family’s funeral home.It wasn’t that Nonie feared the dead. In fact the opposite was true. She sees them—literally, and had been seeing the dead ever since she can remember. She tried telling her parents about it when she was a child, but they’d vehemently dismissed it to imaginary friends, so she’d stopped talking about it. There were only two people who actually knew Nonie’s secret and knew she was the real deal. One was Buggy Mouton, her best friend since kindergarten, and the other was Guy Skinard, whom Nonie considered her one true love since the day she’d laid eyes on him in high school. Buggy now worked at MeeMaw’s Café off of Main in Clay Point, and Guy, as usual, remained ever at her side—the only difference being he’d died in a freak boating accident nine years ago. Still the prankster even in death, Guy loved causing chaos, especially with men Nonie occasionally dated. She could only imagine what mayhem he might stir up while mirroring her at the funeral home and mingling with the newly departed.Only hours into the first day of her new job, pandemonium engulfs the funeral home, and much to Nonie’s surprise, it has nothing to do with Guy. It’s brought on by the family they’re serving. Seventy-two–year-old Dover Fontenot, the Major of Clay Point, dies in a car accident after indulging in a little extra-curricular activity—only the activity wasn’t with his wife , Hazel. And when Dover’s mistress, Anna Mae Turner, shows up at the funeral home for the visitation hell’s gates rip open and Broussard’s Funeral Home suddenly turns into a geriatric war zone.Amidst the flying hairpieces, canes and curses, Nonie’s best friend, Buggy, decides to drop by the funeral home with news she can’t wait to share. A cable network in New Orleans wants to put together a paranormal investigation team and have them check out purported haunted locations in Louisiana. For every location with solid filming potential, each member of the team gets a five hundred dollar pay check. And Buggy knows without question that if anyone can sniff out the ‘restless’ dead, it’s Nonie.Knowing nothing about paranormal investigation, but lured by the sound of the adventure and the dollar signs circling through her head, Nonie agrees to become part of the Boo Krewe, the name Buggy has formally given the makeshift team.The lure of money may be one thing, but what Nonie doesn’t realize is that occasionally the dead, so excited that someone can actually see and hear them, decide to follow her home, into her bed—the bathroom—the shower. And adding to this new-found frustration, Nonie barely finishes her first investigation when she gets a visit from Anna Mae Turner—the mayor’s former mistress—now dead, as well, and harassingly insistent that Nonie bring her killer to light.Nonie quickly learns that the ‘gift’ she’s carried since birth is really a giant wart on the butt of life—and no amount of Compound W is going to make it       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4178.Cormac_McCarthy">About Cormac McCarthy</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4178.Cormac_McCarthy"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1593872299p3/4178.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4178.Cormac_McCarthy">Cormac McCarthy
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
17,169
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4338759208101637697">Cormac McCarthy is an American novelist and playwright. He has written ten novels in the Southern Gothic, western, and post-apocalyptic genres and has also written plays and screenplays. He received the Pulitzer Prize in 2007 for <i>The Road</i>, and his 2005 novel <i>No Country for Old Men</i> was adapted as a 2007 film of the same name, which won four Academy Awards, including Best Picture.<br/><br/>His earlier <i>Blood M</i></span>
  <span id="freeText4338759208101637697" style="display:none">Cormac McCarthy is an American novelist and playwright. He has written ten novels in the Southern Gothic, western, and post-apocalyptic genres and has also written plays and screenplays. He received the Pulitzer Prize in 2007 for <i>The Road</i>, and his 2005 novel <i>No Country for Old Men</i> was adapted as a 2007 film of the same name, which won four Academy Awards, including Best Picture.<br /><br />His earlier <i>Blood Meridian</i> (1985) was among <i>Time Magazine's</i> poll of 100 best English-language books published between 1925 and 2005 and he placed joint runner-up for a similar title in a poll taken in 2006 by <i>The New York Times</i> of the best American fiction published in the last 25 years. Literary critic Harold Bloom named him as one of the four major American novelists of his time, along with Thomas Pynchon, Don DeLillo, and Philip Roth. He is frequently compared by modern reviewers to William Faulkner.<br /><br />In 2009, Cormac McCarthy won the PEN/Saul Bellow Award, a lifetime achievement award given by the PEN American Center.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4338759208101637697" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
